Question 1: Dealing with csv file

Q1.1: First, import and read the “items_ordered.csv” file such that the data is cleaned up and ready for analysis, and name it “item_csv.” Examine the data type and determine if the data type should be changed for a certain variable. If so, change the variable’s data type appropriately.

library(readr)
library(dplyr)

Tip

Hint: Note that this CSV file is messy. Open it up in Excel and examine it. Each row has only one column that contains five elements separated by “;”. There is a way to parse them nicely during the importation process by adjusting the setting of read_csv() function. Inside the function, you will want to add appropriate arguments. For details, look up help by typing “?read_csv”. Alternatively, you may use the menu to generate codes to execute (import > dataset > from Text(readr)). After you read the file into R, you may change data types. For instance, “order_date” variable can be nicely converted to date data type using the lubridate package introduced in M07 and M08.

Q2.2: Using the data imported earlier, create a chart showing the count of the quantity of the items ordered. Create a subdirectory called “images” under the project folder such that it is on the same level as “data” subdirectory. Next, save it as several different graphics files, such as png, pdf, and jpeg. Make sure the files are saved into the “images” subdirectory.

library(dplyr)
library(ggplot2)

# Create images folder if it does not already exist
if (!dir.exists("images")) {
  dir.create("images")
}

# Create chart
item_plot <- item_excel %>%
  ggplot(aes(x = item, y = quantity)) +
  geom_col() +
  labs(
    title = "Quantity of Items Ordered",
    x = "Item",
    y = "Quantity"
  ) +
  theme_minimal()

In the end, you should have 5 variables in the data frame. Further, clean up the data by replacing “N/A” with R type missing value (“NA” or “–”) and save the data as “dis_exec_clean”. When successful, you will see red-colored NA in the affected cells. Further, clean up the column names. That is, convert the title format to lowercase and use “_” between words in the variable name.

library(readr)
library(dplyr)
library(janitor)

Attaching package: 'janitor'
The following objects are masked from 'package:stats':

    chisq.test, fisher.test
dis_exec <- read_csv("data/dis_exec.csv")
Error:
! 'data/dis_exec.csv' does not exist in current working directory:
  'C:/Users/user/Desktop/Tasks/Jonathan/Arturo'.
dis_exec_clean <- dis_exec %>%
  clean_names() %>%
  mutate(across(where(is.character), ~na_if(.x, "N/A"))) %>%
  mutate(across(where(is.character), ~na_if(.x, "--")))

Question 4: Web scraping

Q4.1: To begin, the first thing you need to do is to read the target web page URL into R by typing the following in your Rmd file.

url <- “https://web.archive.org/web/20220201012049/https://www.imdb.com/chart/top/”

Next, by following the procedure explained in the video, create a vector called “top_movies,” which is the top 250 movies shown on the web page. When you do this, try to read and create the “top_movies” dataset in one chain of piping operators. Next, clean up the vector by deleting blank rows.

library(rvest)

Attaching package: 'rvest'
The following object is masked from 'package:readr':

    guess_encoding
library(dplyr)
library(stringr)

url <- "https://web.archive.org/web/20220201012049/https://www.imdb.com/chart/top/"

top_movies <- url %>%
  read_html() %>%
  html_elements(".titleColumn a") %>%
  html_text(trim = TRUE) %>%
  .[. != ""]

Tip

Hint: This task is a replica of the example shown in the principles lecture, although the content is different. However, instead of creating multiple file names, we are only interested in the “top_movies” dataset. You need to use functions such as read_html(), html_elements(), and html_text() in order to extract the top movies. The stringr package’s functions will be useful for working with strings.

Q4.2: Now, scrape the year and assign it “year” as a variable name by applying the same procedure used to extract the “top_movies” dataset above. Next, clean the data.

library(rvest)
library(stringr)

url <- "https://web.archive.org/web/20220201012049/https://www.imdb.com/chart/top/"

year <- url %>%
  read_html() %>%
  html_elements(".secondaryInfo") %>%
  html_text(trim = TRUE) %>%
  str_remove_all("[()]") %>%   # remove parentheses
  str_trim()                  # clean spaces

year
  [1] "1994" "1972" "1974" "2008" "1957" "1993" "2003" "1994" "1966" "2001"

Q4.3: Still on this same URL, now scrape the rating and name it “rating” as a variable name. If there is any other extra information, clean the data.

library(rvest)
library(stringr)

url <- "https://web.archive.org/web/20220201012049/https://www.imdb.com/chart/top/"

rating <- url %>%
  read_html() %>%
  html_elements(".imdbRating strong") %>%
  html_text(trim = TRUE) %>%
  as.numeric()

Question 5: Creating a data frame from web-scraped data

Create a data frame called “top250_movies” by combining the three vectors you created earlier – “top_movies,” “year,” and “rating.” Then save the data after sorting it in descending order of rating. Confirm that your work was done correctly by printing the top and bottom 6 rows.

library(dplyr)

# Combine into data frame
top250_movies <- data.frame(
  top_movies = top_movies,
  year = year,
  rating = rating
)

# Convert types (just in case)
top250_movies <- top250_movies %>%
  mutate(
    year = as.numeric(year),
    rating = as.numeric(rating)
  ) %>%
  arrange(desc(rating))   # sort by highest rating

# View top and bottom 6 rows
head(top250_movies, 6)
                top_movies year rating
1 The Shawshank Redemption 1994    9.2
2            The Godfather 1972    9.1
3   The Godfather: Part II 1974    9.0
4          The Dark Knight 2008    9.0
5             12 Angry Men 1957    8.9
6         Schindler's List 1993    8.9
tail(top250_movies, 6)

Tip

Hint: To combine the three vectors, the number of elements in each vector should be the same. Check it first. One way to do it is by passing the vectors in length() function – e.g., length(top_movies). Of course, you can visually inspect the vectors by printing them on the screen using the print() function or simply typing the vector name and executing it. If you converted the vector to a data frame, length() won’t work; use nrow() instead.

Question 6: Replacing values with NA

Use the following code to generate a “data” dataset and use it for Q6. Here our interest is to replace some values and characters with NA.

Tip

Hint: Use “naniar” package that was introduced earlier in the expanded codebook. It has several functions that are useful for this operation: replace_with_na(), replace_with_na_all(), replace_with_na_if(), and replace_with_na_at().

Data

data <- tibble::tribble(
  ~name,           ~x,  ~y,              ~z,  
  "N/A",           1,   "N/A",           -100, 
  "N A",           3,   "NOt available", -99,
  "N / A",         NA,  "29",              -98,
  "Not Available", -99, "25",              -101,

Q6.1: Replace all elements with “N/A” with the proper missing value in R (e.g., “NA”) across all character variables.

library(dplyr)

dis_exec_clean <- dis_exec_clean %>%
  mutate(across(where(is.character), ~na_if(.x, "N/A")))

Q6.2: Replace all elements with “N/A”, “N A”, or “N / A” with a missing value in R (e.g., “NA”) across all character variables. Use the four different functions to do the task.

library(dplyr)

dis_exec_clean <- dis_exec_clean %>%
  mutate(across(where(is.character), ~case_when(
    .x %in% c("N/A", "N A", "N / A") ~ NA_character_,
    TRUE ~ .x
  )))

Q6.4: Out of the four functions you tried, Which one is your favorite function and why?

My favorite function is na_if() because it’s the easiest to use and very straightforward. It does exactly what I need without extra complexity. The other functions work too, but they require more code and are harder to read. na_if() is cleaner and more efficient for this type of task.

indicators_2016 <- WDI(
  country = "all",
  indicator = c(
    gdp_per_capita = "NY.GDP.PCAP.CD",
    population = "SP.POP.TOTL"
  ),
  start = 2016,
  end = 2016,
  extra = TRUE
) %>%
  select(iso3c, country, region, gdp_per_capita, population) %>%
  filter(region != "Aggregates")

Q7.4: Draw a chart that shows the impact of GDP on the student/teacher ratio and do all the things to make the chart presentable. This includes, but is not limited to, mapping population to the size of data points and region to the color of the data points, adding country names next to the data points as a label, transformation of data, addition of linear regression line as well as all the usual labels for x, y, title, subtitle, etc.

library(dplyr)
library(ggplot2)
library(ggrepel)
library(scales)

Attaching package: 'scales'
The following object is masked from 'package:purrr':

    discard
The following object is masked from 'package:readr':

    col_factor
# Join datasets
combined_data <- s_t_ratio_2nd_edu_2016 %>%
  left_join(indicators_2016, by = c("country_code" = "iso3c"))

# Check column names
names(combined_data)
 [1] "edulit_ind"     "indicator"      "country_code"   "country.x"     
 [5] "year"           "student_ratio"  "flag_codes"     "flags"         
 [9] "country.y"      "region"         "gdp_per_capita" "population"    
# Create a clean country label column
# Change country.y below to country.x if needed
plot_data <- combined_data %>%
  mutate(country_label = country.y) %>%
  filter(
    !is.na(gdp_per_capita),
    !is.na(population),
    !is.na(student_ratio),
    !is.na(country_label),
    gdp_per_capita > 0
  )

# Plot
ggplot(plot_data, aes(x = gdp_per_capita, y = student_ratio)) +
  geom_point(aes(size = population, color = region), alpha = 0.7) +
  geom_text_repel(aes(label = country_label, color = region), size = 3, max.overlaps = 20) +
  geom_smooth(method = "lm", se = TRUE, color = "black", linewidth = 1) +
  scale_x_log10(labels = dollar_format()) +
  scale_size_continuous(range = c(2, 10), labels = comma) +
  labs(
    title = "Impact of GDP per Capita on Student-Teacher Ratio in 2016",
    subtitle = "Secondary education student-teacher ratio across countries",
    x = "GDP per Capita (log scale, current US$)",
    y = "Student-Teacher Ratio",
    color = "Region",
    size = "Population",
    caption = "Source: World Bank WDI and student-teacher ratio data"
  ) +
  theme_minimal() +
  theme(
    plot.title = element_text(face = "bold", size = 16),
    plot.subtitle = element_text(size = 11),
    legend.position = "right"
  )
`geom_smooth()` using formula = 'y ~ x'

Q7.5: This time, create a separate chart by region. There should be six charts. Also, do all the appropriate coding to improve the aesthetics of the chart.

library(dplyr)
library(ggplot2)
library(ggrepel)
library(scales)

# Join datasets
combined_data <- s_t_ratio_2nd_edu_2016 %>%
  left_join(indicators_2016, by = c("country_code" = "iso3c"))

# Create clean plotting data
# If country.y does not exist, replace it with the correct country-name column
plot_data <- combined_data %>%
  mutate(country_label = country.y) %>%
  filter(
    !is.na(gdp_per_capita),
    !is.na(population),
    !is.na(student_ratio),
    !is.na(region),
    !is.na(country_label),
    gdp_per_capita > 0
  )

# Faceted chart by region
ggplot(plot_data, aes(x = gdp_per_capita, y = student_ratio)) +
  geom_point(aes(size = population, color = region), alpha = 0.7) +
  geom_text_repel(
    aes(label = country_label),
    size = 2.5,
    max.overlaps = 15,
    show.legend = FALSE
  ) +
  geom_smooth(
    method = "lm",
    se = FALSE,
    color = "black",
    linewidth = 0.8
  ) +
  scale_x_log10(labels = dollar_format()) +
  scale_size_continuous(range = c(2, 8), labels = comma) +
  facet_wrap(~ region) +
  labs(
    title = "GDP per Capita and Student-Teacher Ratio by Region (2016)",
    subtitle = "Secondary education student-teacher ratio across countries",
    x = "GDP per Capita (log scale, current US$)",
    y = "Student-Teacher Ratio",
    size = "Population",
    color = "Region",
    caption = "Source: World Bank WDI and student-teacher ratio data"
  ) +
  theme_minimal() +
  theme(
    plot.title = element_text(face = "bold", size = 16),
    plot.subtitle = element_text(size = 11),
    strip.text = element_text(face = "bold", size = 10),
    legend.position = "bottom"
  )
`geom_smooth()` using formula = 'y ~ x'
